I'm fine with investing a substantial amount of time in a game, but I want to be rewarded for it.  Some games it feels like you're just beating your head against a wall for hours before you finally concede.  This is a downside to the otherwise good house rule where the heroes can have negative conquest tokens.  

My like of this game has decreased somewhat, given that playing 4e DnD has made me realize I actually don't really care for tactical combat all that much.  I'd much rather play Heroquest or Warhammer Quest.

RATING: 6.5
My play of this game has been more than once, but in general, it's just too over wrought to be the kind of game I want to play and own. As a dungeon romp, it delivers most all that you want: specific 'story' elements, lots of miniatures based combat, load of items. I don't personally like that one player plays the Dungeon Master and is opposed to the others. It feels like lazy design - the DM player just doesn't have as much to do because unlike regular role playing games, this one is pre-fab leaving the DM character just there to wreck havoc. In the end, I don't want a board game of RPGs. I'd much rather play a true RPG and be done with it.

RATING: 6
2008-08-05
Better gameplay than in its sibling, Doom the Boardgame. Still, I prefer Doom because of the theme and simpler rules. How you experience these two games is highly dependent of the dungeon master. It's on the DM's responsibility to make the theme present. In this way Descent and Doom resemble role playing.

2016-03-03
Descent takes the players for a potentially epic adventure of monster slaying and looting. The game feeds the most basic human cravings for killing your enemies and getting richer. 

Descent is also a very ambitious venture trying to simulate an action role playing game in a boardgame format. It takes quite a lot of effort to manage all the rules, unique items, character builds, spells, events etc. Throw in an expansion or two and you find yourself amidst mountains of plastic and cardboard. All this translates into a very consuming gameplay for something that should be fast and jampacked with action on an instinct.

Here the role of the dungeon master (usually the owner of the game) is crucial. He has to keep the players from analysing their moves too much. He is also the most important person at the table for the story telling and immersion. If you find yourself just pushing plastic and rolling dice instead of imagining yourself running in a dungeon with a sword in your hand, you know that you are missing the best part.

With the right group, Descent can be great fun. Play on a gut feeling and forget analyies. Clinch fists and throw yourself into an adventure.

RATING: 6.9
For those who have fond memories of HeroQuest from their youth, this game is like HeroQuest on steroids.  Suffers from overly long playtimes (6-8 hours or more, unless you play it all the time and remember all of the rules, official rulings, FAQs, and errata off the top of your head).  Suffers also from FFG's obvious lack of thorough play-testing (particularly when expansions are added on). Suffers also from fairly cheesy storylines. Also anti-climatic in that by the time the heroes reach the end of the quest, they are so buffed as to be able to take out the largest dragon with one swipe. 

The components are awesome, though, and the sheer amount of stuff that the game allows for is great (yes, we've got rules if you are stunned! or dazed! or poisoned! or are walking through mud! or lava! or on ice! or if you are standing on top of some mushrooms!).  I prefer the art direction here to the second edition, where it became a bit too cartoony/anime for my taste (in addition to having some of the cooler elements of the game removed in the dumbing down of it all).

I have hopes that I'll get another chance to play this when my kid is older and when I have a dedicated game room that I can shut the cats out of so that we can play this in little pieces over a number of evenings.

RATING: 6
While the premise is enjoyable, the game is muddled and weighed down with things that serve no purpose. Why add loads of rock and hole tokens to the game when they could have been printed on the tiles? Why not give everyone a character sheet that can be written on rather than handing out a token for every stat that is raised? Why not handle money another way besides piles and piles of easy-to-lose chits? On top of all that, the game is slow. It feels unfair when the Overlord gets an advantage and every tiny action you do can be countered with summoning more monsters. This is one game that would benefit from slimming down and being more accessible, the rule book is twice as big as it needs to be. Still fun, but not as much as it could have been.

RATING: 6
I played this game just once, so these are mostly impressions that may change after multiple plays.

Descent is the first dungeon crawler I've ever tried. It's a genre that never cought my interest, because I always thought that I would rather play an RPG instead of one of these. I have to say, that I liked it better than I expected. It's an interesting concept, but still not my cup of tea. 

To start, Descent is a game with a LOT of luck and it's also really long... and for me the two things really don't get along very well with each other. I don't want to play for 3-4 hours and depend on card draws and dice rolls so much. Of course there's a lot that a game like this can offer: Campaigns, different missions, scenarios, characters that get stronger and better! This is all interesting, but I think that for all of this a RPG works better. But most of all Descent doesn't give me what I search for in a boardgame. I miss the mechanics, the though choices to make, the planning and the feeling of achievement. Dice can't give you this. I know this game is all about the experience, but I don't like games that are focused on theme so much to forget to put real mechanics in.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Very fun dungeon crawl game that's only drawback is playing time. But even playing time can be fairly variable depending on the scenario and the number of players/heroes. The rules aren't overly complicated for a game like this to the extent that it is pretty easy to get a new player up to speed quickly with what they need to know to function at a basic level and then just introduce more specific rules as the situations come up. The stock scenarios have good variety with each tending to introduce something unique (monsters, tactics etc.). There are also a lot of well done, interesting fan created scenarios on the Fantasy Flight website.

While there are a fair amount of rules this is a game that can be picked up quicker than you might expect. I'd recommend the more experienced player play as the overlord if you want the game to flow along at a nice pace. For new overlords out there be sure to play a couple of solo games prior to running a group of heroes through a scenario, especially if they will be new players. This will help you get the rules down well enough that the game shouldn't bog down too much. You'll want to be able to explain each players options for each turn at least for the 1st room or so.  

There are a large number of expansions available for the game including a book of new scenarios. That combined with the fan created scenarios on FFG's website gives the game a huge replay factor. I'd count the Well of Darkness expansion as a must have since it provides several interesting monsters that work well with less experienced players. Weak monsters that still have the ability to hurt you are great for scaling adventures for different numbers of players and for players of varying levels of experience. The addition of expansions that introduce campaign play (Road to Legends and Sea of Blood) takes the game to another level. If you want to play the game a lot then these expansions offer an easy way to accomplish this.

RATING: 8
An enjoyable and well produced dungeon crawl with rules that are a good take off the DOOM system. The problem is that most games tend to play as follows:
-Heroes struggle for hour 1-2
-Heroes get better items and are a good match for the Overlord for hour 2-3
-Heroes get amazing items and the Overlord is outmatched for hours 3-4

Which leads to the next problem: when the Overlord does win, it can be extremely unsatisfying for all players. I've also noticed that all players are very interested and pumped to play for the first 2-3 hours, but after that it just seems like everyone wants to finish the game and push it off the table.

A great game, but not without it's flaws.

RATING: 7
This is the first rating I've ever given a game that's been higher than a 9. I've only played four times, but I'm sure Descent players know that 4 plays is equivalent to several hours of gaming.

I love everything about this game, but that is not to say that it is not with out its flaws. What pushes this game into the 9 and above category for me is that the flaws do not ruin the game for me. I don't get the same feeling when I play this game that I do when I'm playing any other game in my collection. I love playing Le Havre, but it is not as exciting as when I'm rolling to hit at a critical point in the game. I love the action cards in El Grande, but they definitely do not compare to drawing and playing the wonderful Overlord cards.

I love managing my resources in euros, but I never feel as tense as when I am managing the small number of resources that I get in Descent. I don't feel as satisfied in making clever moves in other games than when I make a clever move in a game of Descent. Clever moves come with flash in this game. They come with flash, luck, excitement, theme, and above all else, a wonderful game.

It's the best game I have in my collection. I want to play it all the time.

RATING: 8
I could write so much about this game. The pieces and miniatures really appeals to me. And it's a cooperative game when playing with more than 2 players.

Still it's got a few flaws, and it's length makes this a week-end only game. It differs a bit from D&D in the way that the characters does not evolve/level up. It's the gear that makes the difference. That way it's more a strategy game, than an adventure game.

The biggest flaw is the balance when playing the standard missions or the expansion missions. With 2 heroes (2-3 players) it's almost impossible to survive, especially the big red non-named bosses. When playing with 3-4 heroes the game becomes too easy until they meet the big red ones. But then they do them faster than just two. So the 2-3 monster cards should been a bit lowered, and the 4-5 should have been upped.

RATING: 5
I quite enjoy a dungeon crawl and there's quite a lot to enjoy about this one.  The Doom system adapts well to a fantasy setting and is easy for new players to pick up.  The plastic figures are really well done. 

There are also some negatives.  Magic isn't well differentiated from physical combat; it is just another way of zapping things.  There is also a lot of down time with a large group. Those are criticisms that can be made of many games in this genre.  My big problem with Descent is that it takes so-o-o-o long.  I'm left thinking that we could have done some actual role-playing instead.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
I used to play this game a lot. One of my very first 'bigger' games. It looks cool with a those minis, dungeon map and everything.
The beginning feels good, really thematic. It is a nice dungeon crawl flair with exploration feeling. The heroes are eager to discover new rooms, enemies, treasure.

But it wears off pretty soon (after ~60 minutes of playing, not counting setup). Tasks becomes repetitive: Try and get to the new room, slay monsters, try to be faster than the newly spawned monsters. Dungeon layout is often designed so you have to run back and forth (get key from there, move back etc.). While this might be fun once, twice or thrice is not. There are major turning points whenever the heroes get a new item tier (silver, gold). If the heroes got their gold gear, the dungeon master can pretty much throw away all cards that are not buffed by permanent boosting cards. But you can also decide to play these cards. It will make the game last longer but not more fun (fun in this games depends on the master being not a dick...).
The boss fights are always luck driven. Who rolls better. After hours of playing, this is frustrating.

If you're the one owning the game, you will end up being the master most of the time. Owning the game isn't that much fun.
I would like to test the second edition though.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8

A good dungeon crawler game. Complicated set up and rules are a little on the heavy side thus making a first time play through a somewhat cumbersome affair.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
If you want a dungeon crawl enough to overlook some warts then this is a good purchase.  You get lots of great components and a flawed but workable system.

If you have no particular attraction to a dungeon crawl then you get a rule book that's too long, a playing time that's too long, and rule system that can too easily become impossible for the heroes or impossible to stop the heroes.

The last part, I think, is the biggest problem.  The game doesn't always scale well.  We've had games where the heroes fell behind the power curve and just got swamped in a horde of enemies that regenerated as fast as we could kill them.  And we've had game were we grabbed a few powerful items and proceeded to kill nearly every enemy before it had a chance to move.

But I gave it an '8' because I have a group that really wants to play a dungeon crawl and is willing to work with the system (house rules) to make an enjoyable experience.

If you don't have that kind of interest, then stay away.

RATING: 8.5

This means GM is able to "win" by killing the characters without accusations of unfairness!  Seems reasonably balanced to both sides, and the characters have to get a move on to win

Beautiful and sturdy components and a lot of creature models.

Decided to buy this game to celebrate getting a new job after 6 months without work. Went to my FLGS in Reading only to find it was out of stock (and out of stock with FFG).  Aaarggh!  Drove to Oxford, where a friend had spotted a copy for sale, only to find the shop was closed.   Aaargh!  Luckily shop opened at 10.30am (!) and managed to buy the only copy there. RATING: 5
This game was not what I was hoping it would be.  It has the characters and monsters from Runebound and is an ok adventure game but I feel like it should have had more character development like Runebound.  The items and monsters are designed in such a way that you will fight the strongest monster each board you play and have a weapon or item that makes this doable.  This means that the only way to play a campaign or even a small 2 or 3 linked adventures is to discard your items and start over.  You have the same character but you have so little to show for your last adventure.  I have not seen the campaign rules that where recently released but $120 is more than I'm willing to spend to try it out.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I remember when I first saw the enormous box sitting in my local game store "The Gaming Pit" back around 2009 or so. The game looked so intimidating with the dragon hovering over the heroes... I was instantly hooked. Due to the lack of funds, I couldn't purchase the game myself, but the store held nights where we could play the game. We played from 12 AM - 6 AM only to accomplish one quest; sad to say is that the majority of the time resulted in the heroes losing. This game was THAT hard. We didn't mind losing. The game immersed us in its realm, its story, and the fact that the overlord can seem like he is on your team one minute as an aid can backfire the next time you blink. This game felt much more than a game. It felt like a story unraveling without having to read. I highly recommend this game for the 2.0 release with the conversion kit. You are playing as a hero in a party attempting to overthrow the overlord who controls monsters trying to smash your faces in and devour the corpses. Different quests allow for different scenarios, monsters, and events to occur. An active imagination and roleplaying is encouraged while playing. I loved this game.

RATING: 7
Nem vagyok egy nagy fantasy fanatikus, de ezt a játékot régóta ki akartam próbálni. Nemcsak mint az "egyik legnagyobb játék a boltban":) keltette fel az érdeklődésemet, hanem a fantasy-s ismerősök beszámolói alapján is kíváncsi voltam rá.
A szabályrendszer nem túl bonyolult, ez kellemes meglepetés. A játék maga viszont elég hosszúra sikerült (de nem árultak zsákbamacskát, gyárilag is 4 órát írnak).
Sikerült olyan pályát (5.) kiválasztanunk, amit teljesen esélytelennek éreztem, mégis nagy élmény volt, mert így szinte mindenbe belekóstolhattam, amit az alapjáték nyújthat (nálam tapasztaltabbak megerősítettek ebben). A taktikai elemek (teleport, lépcső, stb.) tetszenek, a szerencse szerepe nem volt bosszantóan nagy. A játék kivitelével is elégedett vagyok: mind mennyiségre, mind minőségre. I absolutely _adore_ this game.  I usually play with both currently available expansions (WoD, AoD) which seems to provide a nearly hopeless situation for the heroes, but I always enjoy the game nonetheless.

The game has a myriad of "flaws" in my eyes:

1) Game balance is very hard to get "right"
2) Scenarios can take a very long time, way longer than exploring 5-6 areas should
3) The spawning mechanic is cool, but its kind of boring for the heroes to always ensure they're staring into openness.  Also, the nearly infinite spawning capabilities seem to really drag out the game sometimes.

I could go on, but what's the point?  Do any of them detract from my enjoyment of the game?  No.

Best dungeon crawl out there!

RATING: 8
I think of all the adventure games I've played, Descent captures best the feeling of the standard dungeoncrawl.  Not very much in the way of character development (or not at all): the goal is to Kill Stuff, Not Die (much), and Complete a Mission.  This sort of "RPG" was never the kind that needed a GM who was willing to soften some blows, but a sadistic GM who kept everyone on their toes (but stayed within the rules of the system) was great.

As a result, Descent works extremely well for what it is.  Plus there are a lot of minis and a nice dungeon structure, which is a notch better than graph paper and little folded cardboard monsters (though I know that much better dungeon crawl bits are out there in general).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
Since I was raised on fantasy rpgs, dungeoncrawls really appeal to me.  So does the concept of lots of stuff in the box.  THis game however seems a bit. . . off.

THere's a rule that says when one player opens a chest, EVERY player gets treasure regardless of where their character is.  This ok with the bronze treasures, but some of the silver and gold are extremely powerful.  We recently played a scenario where there were only silver and gold treasures.  To compensate, the overlord had an invincible monster that could only be killed by an item later in the dungeon.  Didn't help him.  Players got a gold level ruin that with enough surges, could clear most any room in one shot.  Only the demons took more than one hit to kill with this thing, and we had several similar ruins thanks to the every player drawing treasure rule.  

In the end, every single person at the table agreed the Overlord could not win no matter how well he played.  This was not the first time this has happened, but it's the most extreme.

This could probably be fixed by either limiting treasures to the player that opened the chest or by giving the players a set amount of lives like doom does, rather than a pool of tokens that refreshes everytime they find a town portal or open a chest.

RATING: 7
As a solid dungeon crawl, Descent does its best to recreate the atmosphere and feeling of a fantasy roleplaying session while at the same time leaving out all the roleplaying bits: it doesn’t play much different than D&D’s fourth edition but at least Descent doesn’t pretend to be an RPG. 

Fun as far as a boardgame goes, Descent has only a few drawbacks: it’s slow, repetitive and tends to last too long. [i](October 2011)[/i]

[b]Update[/b] - Over the years I’ve grown tired of most dungeon crawls: I’m still attracted to them by their mere theme - as an old roleplayer, I’m quite fond of dungeoning - but the way they play bothers me, as everything boils down to rolling a few dice and checking whether a special power activates or not. What’s still worse though is, they have the bad habit of crawling way too slow and turning into long, endless sessions of repeating the same few actions over and over.

This is why during my Fall 2015 rating revise I lowered my rating from 9 to 7: Descent is still better than Heroquest. Well, perhaps it’s one of the best in the whole genre as well.

RATING: 9
One of the best games I've played, period!  I love the theme, and the mechanics are excellent.  The rules do have some interaction issues at times, but this is something my group has no problem dealing with.  The games can tend to be 4+ hours on many of the quests, which can be an issue at times, but the Road to Legend expansion helps alleviate this to some degree.

The rules can be quite involved, and the game could be quite daunting to new boardgamers.  If you're new to boardgaming, a good game to play before this game is DOOM:the boardgame as it's a somewhat similar implementation of the same base game engine, with some of the complexity removed, and a somewhat faster playtime.

It's pretty rare for the heroes to beat a quest the first time, or even the second time on some of the more involved quests.  We play the same quest until the heroes win, so we're getting plenty of play time out of the official quests.  I can't recommend this game highly enough, it's great.

RATING: 5
Similar in feel to DOOM, but I prefer the latter. Main problem is game length which is much too long, I'd prefer it with less 'restarts' after player death and make the monsters a bit easier to balance, in fact balance is a bit of an issue too - some characters/ability combos seem much much better than others.

I think the 'GM' player is the key here. All the games of Descent I've played the GM seemed to think their role was to be a GM, move the story along and give the players a fun time ... end result, all the players are ubermunkins tooled up for stomping the monsters. Scenarios play like that too, hence the volume/level of the nasties at the end. Better if the scenarios are tougher, the GM plays 'nasty' and the players have to make do with less powerups etc.

Overall I much prefer DOOM and that's probably because a GM in Doom gets the idea 'kill the marines' and the game is better for it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
Thie game is more Fantasy Flight junk. Which is a shame, because this is yet another game of theirs that should be good; not great perhaps, but at least good. But it's not, and the reason - wait for it - is pacing. You spend *so* much time in this game doing ultimately pointless and meaningless activity, the whole enterprise is compromised. Waste a few Kobolds, waste 15 minutes of my life. Kill some Naga, kill 20 minutes. You spend far, far too much time dealing with monsters who aren't a serious threat and taking a couple points of damage; as a result, the game goes on far past its expiration date. For comparison purposes, I think the combat here is actually significantly more tedious and less interesting than just playing D&D or some other d20 game, and I don't see why you wouldn't just do that instead. Or the vastly superior Battlestations.

Anyway, there is some fun on offer here. Just not enough to fill the box.

I'm a qualified fan of Doom: The Boardgame, and I think what that game has over Descent is the pressure on ammo and somewhat tighter pacing. In Doom, you're always worried about your ammo supply as well as wounds and there is pressure to keep moving, and far more of the monsters are legitimate threats to the players. Plus, the cardplay in Doom for the "invader" player is a lot more interesting and suspenseful for the marines (and more thematic ... too many Overlord cards in Descent have gamey rather than flavorful effects). In Descent, there are too many boring Master cards, too many unthreatening monsters, and too much general screwing around and not enough playing a game.

Now, having just trashed the game, there is one condition in which I would play again: if I could be gaurenteed a game that would complete in 3 hours tops.  Doom was a bad game salvaged by a good expansion, because the scenarios in the expansion seemed  well-designed, which the ones in the basic game were not. If Descent could have a similar tightening of its scenarios, getting rid of a lot of the junk, it could be fun. But as it is now, it's not.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
My gaming group absolutely loves this game.  It probably stems from a love of D&D, but never enough time (or consistent show up) to get a real campaign rolling.  This game solves that with all the action, enough theme to tie it all together, and plenty of mayhem in a box.  Character selection can make a big difference in this game, and plenty is left up to chance, but it is usually very intense from start to finish.

My problems with this game:  the single game length can still be several hours, I'd prefer about 2, rather then 3 1/2 to 4.  The other problem is that it can turn really brutal.  For the overlord to win, he needs to hammer the weakest player, repeatedly.  For the players, they really stomp the crap out of the overlord, making him feel powerless when the players are winning.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.75
The only thing I really have to say bad about this game is the length.  If you have half a day (or whole day) to kill and are into the dungeon crawler games then this is for you most definitely.

The miniatures are great (though many of the bases may be warped; to fix that just stick them in boiling water for about 30 seconds and bend them straight and dump them in ice cold water).  I would have liked to see the character sheets a bit more durable (like those in Last Night On Earth).  The combat mechanic is nice.

The first few times of playing you'll most likely find yourself referring to the instructions every 10-15 mins and that can get boring but once everyone knows what they're doing it gets better, runs smoother, and doesn't take as long.

Overall, the game is great.  It's just the length it takes to play that takes a lot out of it for me.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
The first dungeon crawling game I ever truly loved. Honestly in some ways, it's still my favorite.

The Pros:

-Playing just one scenario is truly satisfying and gives you a complete dungeon crawling experience WITH PROGRESSION. This is something no dungeon crawler since, even Descent 2.0, has managed to get right. The escalation as both sides grow in power feels epic. This is what drew me to the game in the first place and what makes it work for those of us who don't have the time to devote to regularly play an RPG.
-Fatigue is an excellent mechanic. Being able to strategically spend some limited resource just to get an extra square of movement or activate a cool ability or add a power die is great.
-Surges are also a cool mechanic. Adds more fun and variation to rolls.
-Playing as the Overlord is fun!
-Characters are varied and interesting.

The Cons:

-Can be lengthy. This is one of the few games I will sit down and devote 2-3 hours to, but now that I've played so many games of it that's less appealing.
-The balance is pretty fucked in the base game. Don't expect to win if you're the Overlord - your monsters suck too much. Except in a couple scenarios where the Heroes don't have a chance. Ugh.
-There's a couple things that are a little fiddly. Things like the different types of actions (move, run, especially readying) are unintuitive.

RATING: 8
4 hours of "knock your socks off" dungeoning without having to purchase all those darn D&D rulebooks.  Overall, a well done game, with lots of bits, and great plastic monsters.

Edit:  I upped this to a nine after adding the [gameid=22361] expansion.  The game plays better with more stuff, and the extra ability to craft the overlord deck to your liking adds a lot of value.

Second Edit:  Since I have now added [gameid=29298] to my Descent collection, all of the Descent products that I own have been moved to 10.  This game is now considered complete due to the integration of Road to Legend.  Without a doubt, this game is the cream of the crop among its genre.  One of my favorites, and one of the best.

EDIT: 08/05/16 - I edited this down to an "8".  I love the game, but it has been sold off in a flea market because it sat unplayed for nearly 8 years.  There just wasn't any time to play, I have other games that I want to play, and it was just too big.  But I love the game, and if someone else was wanting to run a "campaign" (as that is really what this game is all about), and I knew I could dedicate the time to it (like a good D&D campaign), then I would jump back in in a heartbeat.  As it is, though, I no longer love the game like I used to because I don't have the time to dedicate to it like I used to.  Otherwise, if I were younger, and had different gaming friend that were more "D&D'ish" and less "Eurogamish", then I would play it more!

EDIT:  08/23/16 - After having talked to a gaming buddy that owns "Second Edition", and realizing that the new ap called "Road to Legend" plays the Overlord for you, I can now play Descent solo!  Woo hoo - probably the best possibility for me to play this game is solo, so now the "Second Edition" is the new kid on the block for me.

RATING: 4
I've got no problem rating games after one play. For Descent, let's start at a 7. But, admittedly, ratings for this game probably mean more after a few plays to see how different games and scenarios unfold.

For a game that is supposed to last hours, this was less fiddly than I expected. Then again, I was the newbie running a hero, and I didn't have the burden of all the rule subtleties. What do I like? I like the cooperative aspect of working with other heroes. I like that heroes play differently. I like that line of sight matters, and the range/charge/damage dice are pretty cool. 

What don't I like? Any game with a massive rulebook can be a bit clunky. I suspect true Ameritrash fans relish in immersing themselves in rules complexity, whereas I tend to appreciate streamlined rules yet deep play. We got utterly crushed in our first game, but I don't mind... that can happen when you're rolling gobs of dice. Ultimately, I still felt that my choices mattered, and that will certainly keep me open to trying this one again. I'll still choose Heroscape for the wonderful plastic and truly unique and absurd selection of army options, but for exposing me to some good ol' Ameritrash, this ain't half bad. 

Update: Given that there's a new cleaned-up version of the game, I'd be willing to try the new version once again.

RATING: 8
Better than Doom, but doesn't unseat Warhammer Quest as the ultimate dungeon crawl game (in my estimation).  There is more variety in Warhammer Quest, which I greatly enjoy, though at the cost of slightly higher complexity.  Graduating from Warhammer Quest to role-playing proper is also much more fluid, which I appreciate as a GM.  That being said, Descent is a fun game.  The length is indeed long, but not all that long for the genre it's in.  Familiarity with the game also speeds it up quite a bit.  I found it more enjoyable than doom, which partly has to do with theme, and partly a few improvements in terms of the card management for the "Overlord" player along with more flexibility in how improvements can be garnered for the characters.  If you're into Dungeons & Dragons, it's an even better deal, since all the miniatures and terrain are the correct scale and work very well for said game.  I just finished painting up the Hill Giant and will certainly use it in my D&D games.  Having web tokens, cone flame templates, etc, is also cool.  So, I had fewer reservations buying it because I knew I could get mileage out of the miniatures regardless of gameplay.  But, I find the gameplay fun and engaging.

RATING: 4
Edit- played with the expansions a few times and I'm ready to give this one a two-point bump.  The expansions fill out the game quite a lot (while adding even more bells and whistles, which the game arguable doesn't need), allowing for more variety not only in terms of in-game action but also in terms of potential scenario design.  The new monsters, traps, heroes, and equipment make for a lot of variety and play styles which is a good thing.  I still think the game is too slow and poorly paced- if this could come down to a regular 2 hour game it could be one of the great dungeon crawl/adventure games.

Original comment follows:
A game that wants so badly to be great that it's depressing to watch it fall so short.  It is indeed like a pumped-up, more complex version of Heroscape but with an exaggerated playtime and about 30% too many bells and whistles.  It's probably a better game than DOOM in the long run, but it's functionally the same thing with more window dressing.  I'd rather play the D&D board game or Mutant Chronicles for this sort of the game.  I was disappointed in the overall lack of atmosphere or story and I found that toward the last quarter of every game I've played that everyone's interest and enthusiam tends to wane as it turns into a hack-and-slash slog to finish the scenario.  It isn't bad at all, really, just bloated and more boring than it ought to be given all the awesome components and some neat mechanical ideas.  If you're the overlord, say everything in a Bill Cosby voice and the game is HILARIOUS.

RATING: 10
Awesome blend between a tactical game and a [u]dungeon crawler[/u] - one player controls the monsters and the others the heroes. Wonderful miniatures & huge production value (80 minis - half are big size, 500+ tokens, hundreds of cards, modular map, tiles etc.)! Has a free scenarios PC editor. Easy to make custom scenarios for this game.
But it's hard to bring it to the table because the high number of components and finding a group of players can be hard.
Suitable for [color=red]solitaire[/color] games using a variant posted on BGG.
Better than "Descent v2" which is a simplified version of Descent and has only half of minis.
Mayday sleeved.

RATING: 7
I did not expect to like the game but I do! I was afraid that it would feel like an oversimplified RPG dungeon crawl instead of a boardgame. Well, it does not because one fundamental difference: the overlord is not a neutral arbiter but tries to crush the heroes as best as he/she can. 

The game looks good, especially the miniatures. (rating +1). The length of a full game is an issue: ~5 hours is a bit too much (rating -1). The biggest problem of the game, however, is its "downhill" structure: it is too difficult for the heroes in the beginning but ridiculously easy in the end game (after they have drawn good treasures). In all the games I have played so far the final fight with the boss is not the culmination of the game but a foregone conclusion: the boss is usually killed before it can take a single turn. (rating - 2). Finally, the game has not been sufficiently tested and balanced: for instance, the threat cost of some of the overlord's cards is clearly off (compare Explosive Rune and Beastmen War Party); some characters are much better than others etc. (rating -1) Despite all this, I do enjoy the game both as the overlord and a hero.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4

With 4 heroes, their various abilities and strengths are able to work together based on positions, enhancing the group in ways that let the player be creative about the battles. Not very creative, but some.

RATING: 7.75
This one's good, it just takes way too long.  If the players are at least mostly focused, turns can move fairly quickly.  But, like any big game like this, distractions can slow it down a LOT and you can easily find yourself in 6+ hour territory.  After that much time, the lack of persistent rewards is especially anti-climactic.

Also, it might be tempting for traditional pen-and-paper DM's to play nice as the Overlord, but it seems like a bad idea with this one.  It's really easy to be too nice, as the player characters are fairly buff or can get that way quickly, and if the players get bored you're in the danger zone for drag again.

Supposedly, the Road to Legend expansion addresses these issues, but I haven't used it yet.

RATING: 7

I think the nature of a good crawler should be like Nethack -- moving through lots of different rooms/levels/experiences, rather than a long-drawn-out tactical combat scenario that doesn't in the end provide enough variety or enough character development. I'm keeping this one on my shelf, and still hoping to like it more in future. But I haven't been motivated to buy any of the expansions, and I'm not hard to sucker into buying expansions....

I'm getting back into this game, wanting it to be good, but starting really basic at vanilla Descent, again. RATING: 5.5
Fun game of heroes hacking their way through dungeons.  Hero players play vs. the Overlord, who controls the monsters.  Game is tactical, requires cooperation among the hero players, and has excellent replay value, since a defeat by the Overlord will require the players to try that dungeon again.  However, the downsides of this game are the size and number of pieces, setup time, and most of all, the extended length of time to play.

Ultimately traded away for several reasons.
1. I like, but don't love the game.
2. Doesn't scale well, and I don't like the idea of one player playing more than one hero.
3. Lengthy FAQ and rules clarifications (which would make becoming familiar with the game difficult as I couldn't get the game to the table frequently enough).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Up to four players take on the role of dungeon explorers, and battle it out against the dungeon master player.  Each turn the players can take two actions, which have to be declared at the beginning of their turn - move, battle, or order.  The dungeon master sits there gurgling and giggling over his deck of evil tricks, controlling the beasts in the dungeon.  There is an objective the explorers have to complete in order to win, while the dungeon master is simply trying to deplete the explorers supply of dungeon "conquest" points.  Fantastic production - miniatures, counters and dungeon map pieces - solid dungeon hack mechanics (see Heroquest, Advanced Heroquest, Dungeons and Dragons Boardgame, etc).  Some good tactical decision making involved by all players.  The down side, and it's a biggie, it takes several (4+) hours to play.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This game is fun, but it takes wayyyy too long.  Maybe that's partially because of our careful play nature (clearing every room) and frequent rules questioning due to infrequent play.  I like that the bad guy is less reactive and can take some steps to do things.  The figures and quality of the game is amazing, as one would expect from FFG.  But at some point when playing this, I can't help but feel I'd rather be roleplaying then just plowing through monsters, so I feel less excited about this than I might otherwise.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Despite a large amount of initial skepticism, I have to acknowledge this is a well-produced adventure game. The adventures are great and the special powers are well done. 
 
While not innovative, FF did a great job assimilating mechanisms from a bunch of different old Games Workshop games. The GW dice system saves a lot of charts and hassle once you get used to it. The thematically hokey runes or glyphs or whatever allow for mid-game powering up and prevent player elimination. Best of all is the wholesale porting over of the one-vs-many system from Fury of Dracula, in which the 'Overlord' moving the bad guys has his own challenges to face and resources to manage. All of these put Descent head and shoulders above most similar titles. 
 
Alarmingly absent is any form of campaign system, which will be a huge sticking point with most players who are attracted to creating storylines or history and enriching the experience, and I predict some backlash beacause of this.  Errata aside [yet again FF is guilty of a shameful lack of playtesting and pre-press editing], Descent is a worthwhile investment if you want to have one-night-stands of dungeoncrawling.

RATING: 7
We went through a full campaign of 1st ed back when.  I've always enjoyed it, but it did have a few rough patches.  

Teleporting back to town and going shopping in the middle of the adventure was king of strange and broke up the flow a bit.
The most bizarre part, though, was the monster spawning system.  As the OL accumulated 'threat', he could use it to bring in reinforcements, which he could then move immediately on his turn.  But, he could only place them in out-of-the-way corners where the heroes had no line-of-sight.  So, you were in constant danger of being ambushed by packs of monsters who would appear out of nowhere.  You might think this would increase tension, and it kind of did, but mostly it lead to this repetitive gaming of the system where you try and position your heroes around the room so that they can see all the nooks and crannies.  And if you ever missed one, boom! a bunch of goblins, or spiders, or blood apes start chewing on you.  This system warped the entire way the game was played.  Instead of sticking together as a team, you were always fanning out to cover all the angles.

1st ed was always a bit too complex for its own good.  It was generally good complexity, but no denying that the deluge of cards, chits, and minis made it tough to manage.  I think things like the hand of hero special power cards (introduced in expansions) were kind of overkill.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
Based on 1 - 4 player game and 2 - 2 player games. Best dungeon crawl game I have ever played. I thought Doom was a pretty decent game even with a few problems but this game improves on the system and the variability of the game is much better with all the possible permutations of 20 different characters, skills and items. The first scenario seems a little too easy for the heroes but it is an introductory scenario. We played scenarios 2 ans 3 after that and it was a very close contest each time. The Overlord player has to play a very good game though to hope to defeat the heroes; the game is not very forgiving of bad Overlord play. The componants are all amazing but I really do not understand why FFG never includes separate reference sheets for these games. It is really annoying to have to go in the rules for the rules on abilities and such or to look at the back of the rulebook. All in all, a very good game if you do not mind the 3-4 hours playing time with 4 players.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Would have given this game an 8 if it weren't for a couple of issues.

1.  The length of the game--4 hours???  Try 8-10 if you pause to think even a little bit about strategy between turns.

2.  The game kind of depends on hit and run tactics, which gets a little repetitive.

3.  The game is all but unplayable for two players unless the rules are modified.  The overlord will ALWAYS win.

4.  Good luck reading the adventure text with a straight face.  It's that bad.

That being said, there are a lot of good things about this game.  The combat system kicks ass, the characters are extremely varied, there are interesting treasures, and the modular dungeon layout destroys any claim to HeroQuest being "customizable."

This is one of those games that I would certainly play if offered, but I probably won't bring it up myself mostly because of the time involved.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
I'm updating my comment after playing Star Wars: Imperial Assault that I consider to be the refinement if not perfection of this system. I rate SW:IA a 10 and Descent a 3. 

The crux of the problem with Descent was how the Overlord would activate all his guys at once and pick off one hero (often strolling by others) until he was knocked out. Then repeat. This was THE way for the OL to play every scenario as it worked. And the downtime waiting for him to maximize his 10+ dudes and cards was annoying. It felt imbalanced in terms of both power and fun.

SW: IA uses group activations that fix that balance of power and downtime beautifully. Now, the Imperial player can only activate a subset of his forces. This may be 1-2 powerful guys or 3-4 Average Joe Stormtroopers. The result is more interesting choices for the Imperial player and a more balanced back-and-forth among all the players. 

To further imbalance the fun factor, the OL gets to level up way more than the heroes. After a session, the OL gets  "experience income" (Conquest points) and purchases upgrades, not the heroes. SW: IA fixes this by both sides getting to purchase skill and/or weapon upgrades after a session but with the winning side getting more. How simple of a fix is that?

The result of all this is the OL and the heroes have very different experiences. The OL gets to play cards, play a wide variety of monsters, and make a lot of different decisions about using them each turn, and generally get to spend more Conquest tokens. The heroes plod along with a few dice rolls and only occasionally have enough Conquest to purchase a skill upgrade (and even here several have been nerf'd for the RTL expansion). 

All in all, I'd much rather have the OL's experience for 6 hours than a hero's.  My prediction was that this was the best of breed for its genre at one point but another game will come along at some point and make this obsolete by addressing the flaws identified above. Little did I know that, per my prediction,  SW: IA would come along and fix the flaws I identified above.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
I was deeply, deeply disappointed with Doom, so I was very nervous as I purchased this game on faith, without having played it beforehand.  But everything that failed in Doom has been revitalized in Descent.  Each character is unique and interesting, the tactics dont feel repetitive (yet), the scenarios are entertaining and seem relatively balanced.

So far, each game I've played has ended at the final room, and has been within a die roll or two of going to either side.  Very satisfying, and very different from Doom where it was painfully obviously unbalanced.

The characters become powerhouses by the end of each adventure, which is good because the challenge and resources available to the Overlord player scale at a similar rate.  This makes a single game very satisfying, although those who crave a long character-development pseudo-RPG may be disappointed.  I think the variety of experience each playthrough more than offsets this loss, however.  Each of the hero characters is unique and exciting.  If I wasn't always the Overlord player, I'd want a crack at playing each of them.

Surprisingly, it comes with optional campaign rules which look like they might actually work.  I doubt they'll give the heroquest fans the sense of continuity they crave, as the heros get "reset" between each game, starting out each new adventure better equipped as they continue through consecutive dungeons.

RATING: 8
Note: It moves just fine if the heros use speed and mobility instead of trying to kill everything.  This isn't apparent at first and the game can get bogged down if you are overly cautious.  I read some threads on the Geek to get it all to click.

Not as structured as a traditional board games that have a definite play time and set things to do in a certain order, but its a fun sandbox to play in.  It's very wide open and it allows lots of light tactical action and group interaction.  The quality of the Overlord can have a lot to do with play time and difficulty. All I can say is that its a lot of fun.  Great dice system and awesome theme!  Can't wait to play more.  You need a group who enjoys the theme and understands that it's not a traditional board game and the enjoyment will be group dependent and not always balanced.

It brings back some early D&D memories!

Paint your minis!

RATING: 7
Old-Skool Hero Quest -style monster smashing. Gotta love it. Mechanics are identical to Doom The Boardgame, but Descent has more expansions and thus offers more replayability. Here's a brief list of pros and cons of the vanilla Descent with zero expansions: 

+ Simple rules and mechanics
+ Tons of stuff
+ Easy to learn, especially when teammates help n00bs getting around dungeons
+ Strong theme. Requires an experienced Game Master, though.
+ Adjustable difficulty.
- Setup time from hell. Add the expansions and you'll get storaging from hell too.
- ...And the constant setup & browsing roulette continues during play. The GM has to keep everything close, since one often needs to find proper gear, figure, trap, effect tile. But then again, it's up to the GM to keep the tension high during the dull browsing sessions.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

As the "tank" class who chose to go DPS, i feel that my role tends to be very muddy when it comes to actual scenarios- I find myself wanting to do damage since I am not really able to soak the damage for my teammates. (Since the DM is smart enough to not focus me)

I feel that taking the knight class to relieve offensive pressure from the DM would have been more useful for my allies which frustrates me when I play this game (since I am a true min-maxer at heart, I would rather not deal any damage and prevent all damage from my allies as this role if possible) and I think that was a mistake in character class picking. 

The LoS mechanic is confusing at times and can result in ridiculous situations such as blocking yourself from making a shot. I think it needs to be changed.

The rest of the game is very enjoyable and I enjoy the cooperative aspects- I just wish it wasn't at the expense of whoever ended up being DM- seems too much like a one-sided game as soon as one side picks up speed from the first few quests. My experience has been HEAVILY hero-sided, and that can be bland and dull and end up making me feel bad for the DM.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I played a few campaigns of D2E (Shadow Rune, Labyrinth of Ruin & Shadow of Nerekhall) prior to playing the original D1E with a campaign (Road to Legend).  While I like both games, at their cores D1E and D2E are fundamentally different games.

D1E plays more like a tactical dungeon crawl with an overarching storyline (i.e. go to a dungeon and get treasure, then go to town to heal and buy stuff, and ultimately go kill the final boss).  D2E is more like a lite role playing game in which completing the objectives often trumps defeating enemies (i.e. go investigate what's going on around the battlefield and fight enemies if need be, but complete your mission before the bad guys complete theirs).  Ironically, D2E is more linear in storyline (the winner of one quest determines which quest will be played next), whereas D1E offers a little more freedom as to where your heroes can go next.

Overall, I like both systems for the unique niches they each offer, but I wouldn't necessarily own both games.  I would recommend a Descent edition based strictly on whether you are looking for a more classic dungeon crawl style game (D1E) or a lite RPG (D2E).  Personally, I already own a few dungeon games, so I'm happy to own D2E vice D1E.

RATING: 6
I have to admit up front that I've never been all that impressed with dungeon crawl games in the past. The either were too fiddly, trying too hard to be RPGs, or they were just shallow and boring dicefests.

Descent manages to stay in the middle of the range nicely, and ends up being an enjoyable experience.

I like the different characters, and the lack of charts, graphs, and 'development'. I like the bits, and the dice with their various qualities are just brilliant.

I'm worried that the overlord's only real strategy is just to overwhelm the heros with continous spawning, and the more spawning, the longer the game takes to play. I'm not really fond of the playtimes as it is, but the game is easily packed up and resumed later, so its not too oppressive.

Overall, Descent fills the dungeon crawling, monster killing niche quite nicely, even if the gigantic box doesn't fit my shelves the same way.

Update 2013:
After playing more recent dungeon crawls, Super Dungeon Explore, Claustrophobia, etc, this one isn't likely to ever make it on the table again.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
With the second edition of this on the way, I figured I'd write out my first review on BGG for one of my favorite games in my collection.

This game looks like a dungeon crawler but plays more like you're manipulating a swords-and-sworcery wielding SWAT team. Running and gunning without a plan will allow the overlord to swat down your group without breaking a sweat. Tactical movement - covering angles, staying close together, planning targets - is essential. Combat is dice-rolling, and with all the randomness that dice entail. The unpredictable element just forces you to play more strategically and throws a monkey wrench into your well-laid-out-plan quite often.

I love the asymmetrical nature of the gameplay. Playing as an adventurer turns the game into a tense race towards the end, as lingering anywhere too long will get you killed as surely as forging ahead too quickly. And in contrast, playing as the overlord is an experience not dissimilar from squashing ants with your thumb. Chipping away at the adventurers, timing your attacks and traps, is excellent fun. Most often, however, it comes down to a single strong attack and some unlucky rolls from the adventurers and it's game over. It's very similar to the Diablo series - the weak monsters will barely scratch you, while the stronger ones will punish you mercilessly, killing you multiple times.

With all the rules-mongering and strategic placement going on, however, the atmosphere of the game suffers somewhat. It feels odd to have traditionally stupid monsters act in perfect harmony to out-flank adventurers. Another problem is that in order for the overlord to win, he has to kill the adventurers - any of them - enough times to deplete their pool of tokens. What this leads to is the optimal strategy for overlord is to simply gun for the weakest character, over and over. Every monster, every trap, every spell will target the same adventurer, and every time that character is brought down the adventurers creep closer to game over. The game then becomes no fun for the adventurers. When the overlord isn't using such cheap tactics, however, and is evenly matched with the players, the balance is generally excellent.

The modular board, while adding plenty of replayability, is same-y enough that you feel like you're running through different variations of the same dungeon. Some more variation in the tiles would be nice - but I believe that the expansions fix this problem.

The length of the game can be somewhat overwhelming, and it is complex enough that you have to refer back to the rules often. I'd call it D&D-lite, but it's really not that much lighter in rules - the experience is just more streamlined. The pacing is good, however, and the constant escalation of both player and overlord abilities makes it so that there's plenty going on to sustain interest. Adventurers are constantly getting better and better loot, and the overlord stockpiles enough tokens to pull more and more dangerous tricks out of his/her hat.

It's such a monstrous game - it requires more table space and time than any other I own. But I've had some spectacular sessions of this game, and when everything clicks it's a great game of combat-heavy strategic dungeon crawling. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
OK, plain vanilla Descent is not fun. So, how have we invested over 100 hours on Descent? By using the campaign rules from The Road to Legends expansion. 
I'm super excited about the soon to be released 2nd Edition.

RATING: 7
A tactical dungeon crawler, where one player is the overlord and the other players are heroes trying to solve a quest. It is based on the mechanics of Doom.

I was disappointed at Doom, which is the main reason I haven't looked too closely at this game as they are descendants of each other. Personally I think this game does it right as there are so many frustrating points with Doom.

The game is mostly tactic and there is more strategy in the game than most of its type even if it is resource management. There are several scenarios in the game box and once you have completed them they feel old, but there are expansions and scenarios on the net. The scenarios in the game and expansions seem generally not very balanced and favor either the heroes or the overlord and many are dependent on getting the right heroes and skills. There isn’t much development with the heroes with interesting choices. There will be combos but once they come they will be pretty obvious.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
This is one of those dungeon crawling games. Yes, I know. Each of "those" games has a different feel to it, some work better, some not so well. What this one has going for itself is that it has really, really nice components, and that rolling the many coloured dice is fun. I also like that the heroes are quite diverse, have quite a few tactical and equipment options, and I like that the dungeon master has quite the selection of mean stuff up his sleeve - which he cannot necessarily play it as he would like it. 

It's a hack-and-slay dungeon crawl. It's far from bad at that, but it's also not the most exciting implementation of it. The rule for (teleporting into town from the dungeon) and buying stuff seems a tad unrealistic. Also, the fact that the dungeon master's best action - respawning monsters in unobserved spots of the dungeon - can be severely restricted by heroes who have famililars (and know how to use them to that end), seems quite unbalanced. 

What always gnaws at me whenever I play one of these games is the question why I don't rather play a real RPG. If the game were real quick, it wouldn't matter that much, but it strives to be as facetted as possible for the hero players, which ends up being quite time consuming, while the pay off is insubstantial. I would recommend this one specifically for people in their early teens who might use it as kick off into playing RPGs.

RATING: 7
Disappointing (after 3 games).  Basic idea of the game is nice.  Components are great but two things are seriously bugging me: 

First of all, the Overlord seems to have a severe disadvantage if the heroes are played properly.  As soon as the first major treasure is looted, all monsters but the most powerful are simply cannon fodder which makes many Overlord cards more or less worthless.  As the OL you either win in the first half of the game or you lose.  This wouldn't be a problem if the overall game time wouldn't be so long.  Which brings me to the second problem:

The second problem is the playing time.  Descent *needs* a group of players who think and act *fast*.  Otherwise it is a nightmare.  My group plays very slow.  After more than 6 hours of play, the Overlord (me) had about 40 minutes of playtime whereas the heroes had used more than 5 hours (in which I had nothing to do other than stare at the wall and listen to endless discussions).  This is most probably a problem with my group rather than with the game but be warned: if your group is slow, expect a game of Descent to drag and drag and drag ...

Edit: Raised rating from 6 to 7 after a game with my 10 year old nephew which was tremendous fun.  Apparently this game needs a group who doesn't overanalyze everything.

RATING: 6.5
Aah, Descent. If only it didn't take so much time ... but then, if it didn't, it would miss half the chrome that makes it so splendid to look at. All these great monsters and dungeon tiles and floor tiles and tokens and counters and cards and bits and pieces and chits ... it's a great view to behold.

The gameplay is pretty good, too, although meanwhile I'm attracted to ... slightly less over-the-top chrome. The main disadvantage is that setup takes a long while, and then playing the game can take a long while as well due to both AP and the GM (well, OL) having to pick all the right monsters and cards and effect tokens when they're needed. Requires a well-organized game box.

The game does get better with more expansions, the main sad thing about these is that they're all meant to be playable with only the base game and one expansion ... consequently, there are precious few places (even with RtL and SoB) where monsters and pieces from all the expansions are needed together. In fact, bar the Quest Companion, I don't think I recall one official scenario with bits from all the expansions.

Which is a bit of a pity.

Anyway, if you're looking for a dungeon crawl and have plenty of time to spare, this is the game you want. Despite sometimes gaping imbalances (mostly fixed by the various FAQ though) and long playing time, Descent is a very awesome experience overall, with mechanics that exactly match the theme's requirements for furious and savage action.

RATING: 6
Not a bad dungeon crawl. In typical Fantasy Flight fashion, the game is as carefully balanced as the Tower of Pisa. Fortunately, the game has enough decisions in it to make it entertaining.

The game is more expensive than it seems at first glance: The insert is awful, and there are so many little counters that you might need at any given time that little plastic bags aren't that great an idea. To keep the game even remotely organized, you'll have to buy at least a couple of tackle boxes.

I'd rate it higher if it didn't take an entire evening to finish a game. Even long games like Magic Realm and Die Macher seem short compared to this.

RATING: N/A
Played half a game once.  For whatever reason it makes everyone's eyes bulge out of their heads when they see the massive spray of bits, cards, dice, minis, markers, chits, placeholders, status indicators, and funny-shaped cardboard nib-nobs.  And then they expect me to know all the rules.  By myself.

"Here's a rule for ya—you're dead!  I win!"

UPDATE:  In the end I'm just not a fan of tactical dungeon combat.  I love dungeon crawls—just with the combat and movement more abstracted.  [i]Descent[/i] goes into a level of detail I find laborious.  It is, by all appearances, a great game, and if I had a rabid group of players who dug it I'd go to the effort of learning and playing it.  With no one clamoring for it, it'll have to anchor my game shelves until such a crowd forms...

RATING: 8
First impressions are very postitive.  We played though an introductory custom scenario to get the hang of the game and learn the basic gameflow.  I must say that I already like it much better than Arkham Horror.  I like the way dices are used in Descent -- one roll shows everything you need to know about the battle outcome.  The first game was tense.  We made lots of mistakes on the way and had many questions concerning basic rules, but overall it was a great experience.  The game took 3 hours (for a small 4-room map), but the time flew and we were involved all the time. Once we finished, we could not believe that three hours had passed - it seemed like an hour.  Really engaging game.  

I like the customization factor and the fact that each game is different thanks to different selection of heroes, initial skills, found items, etc.

RATING: 7
One player is the DM, the others all play heroes, and the DM must try for a TPW. With simple rules for movement, attacking, and items, the party moves through the modular dungeon tiles, set out by the DM as per the scenario, and monster spawn, along with treasures, as the party moves throughout.  The DM gains power with each turn, the heroes with each small victory, and the dungeon's end signals the loss of all items. What?  Until you play the campaign expansion, it's always a one-off adventure, with rules for "leveling" up characters in between (items are still lost, however).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Excellent core game that launched a franchise and showed a new era gaming market what kind of enormous value and imagination could be packed into one box. Original appeal was a light weight dungeon crawler alternative to a full RPG with lots more visual appeal (and therefore friendlier to a broader audience). This was one game that inspired me to get back into tabletop gaming and illustrated the "across the board" improvements in print quality, 3D components, storytelling in a modern tabletop game.  Fantasy Flight continues Descent's evolution with 2nd Edition which aimed to furnish a cleaner rule system to allow for quicker set up and play vs. 1st Ed. At the same time 2nd Ed broadened possibilities for player choice and customization. Backward compatible conversion kit exists for 1st Ed core and expansions so I always encourage folks to pick up older 1st Ed stuff on the cheap if they can find it (including this core box). Worthwhile purchase. Recommend.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

--update august 15, 2011--

Still painting the minis. Still very slowly.

As I'm coming up on a quarter of games of this, I'm becoming comfortable enough to start removing a few things from skill decks and treasure decks that just aren't fun or at all valuable. I've house ruled a few things here and there, and I've started thinking about a few things that I'd like to change up a bit. With all the changes, I'm not sure exactly how to rate this. Out of the box? With my changes?

I think as long as I'm still playing the core game (that is to say, without any dramatic changes to what a player does in a turn and with the same goals still in mind), I have to rate this as I play it. So here's a 9 for you, Descent (though out of the box, it really wouldn't be too much lower).

RATING: 7
Descent adds a lot to the Doom concept that both help and hinder the design.  I give it a big bonus for player customization and a slight mark off for preset-up dungeons (it tends to drag down the flavor of exploration and adventuring).  I think the main problem I had with the game was the time requirement, which added another two full hours onto a standard Doom fare of three.

Update:  After playing with the horribly implemented expansion Well of Darkness, I realized the specific points in Descent that I feel are inferior to Doom.  First off there's too big a gap between levels of combat.  Basically, you either smash a monster to bits or can't hurt it at all.  Secondly, the traps aren't strategic.  They just rain down wounds from the sky and there's little to nothing you can do about it.  Thirdly, there's no fight or flight decision.  You are basically forced to fight everything since it is as fast or faster than you are which brings me to the last point: too many spawns.  There are just far too many creatures that can spawn which often creates a situation of zero mobility and stalls out the game for long periods of time.  I'd still play the base game if offered even knowing these potentially fatal flaws, but I would hesitate to implement the WoD expansion.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Unlike D&D and other traditional pen and paper RPGs which are primarily story driven, Descent: JitD is a competitive contest between a team of heroes and an evil Overlord controlling the dungeon.  If you try to play Descent like a traditional RPG, you will be disappointed.  In order to get the most enjoyment from the game, each team (heroes vs. Overlord) must be as ruthless as possible.  This keeps games moving quickly and reduces frustration.  No one likes to have their obvious defeat dragged out for another hour.
That said, time consumption is one of the biggest drawbacks of the game.  It takes about 30 minutes to setup if you are super organized and then takes about 4-6 hours to complete a single quest.  It is good fun, but you have to commit an entire evening to one game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
I will play this game any day. Adrenaline-pumping quests where monsters spawn constantly and you have to keep moving. You get better with more weapons and loot, and the campaign expansions are epic. I think it can be frustrating as a true competitive game (especially the campaign), so as a "competition game" I give the base set a 7/10 and the expansions a 5/10 or less. But play it a bit more like a GM to give the heroes a chance when they struggle and it can be a lot of fun - a 10/10 "experience." I think the second edition is a better game, if the goal is to play competitively, but this one is a more fun experience for me when the OL is willing to lighten up from time to time.

RATING: 6.5
Ideal Players: 4-5

Description: Dungeons and Dragons style dungeon crawling, minus that annoying talking and traveling in between dungeons.

Pro: Captures the essence of D&D combat. Great if you feel like killing something. Cool dice mechanics with range, hits, and surges in different probability on different color dice. Great cooperative experience.

Con: Random dice rolling largely determines success. Requires one player to be the Dungeon Master, fighting against the other players (could be accomplished through rules for how monsters act). Not much strategic depth or creativity with spells.

RATING: 9.5
I'm split on this one.  First of all, I love the game.  The pieces, the one vs everyone else, the fight together or die alone mentality of it.  The problem is that it is too big for what it is.  Games can easily go 6 hours if it is a bigger board, and it eventually just becomes a grind.  

I think i would enjoy this a little more if there were less monsters in the game, but they were much more powerful.  A dragon that is 15 times the size of the plastic hero should not die before getting an attack.  Every time I've played the heroes just seem over-powered for what they face in the maze.

Edited:  It seems like I am getting what I wanted with the Road to Legend expansion with slightly more powerful foes and weaker beginning heroes.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
This game is *huge* and weighs a ton!  You get an enormous amount of high-quality bits and map parts with this game.  This game is similar to HeroQuest in that there is a "game master" that plays the role of the monsters on the board.  Unlike HeroQuest, the game master could be considered a player; he can purchase and play cards to hurt the the other players, and will lose the game if the other players win.  This would be a great game if the play time was about 1/2 as long; part of the reason why this game is so long is that the game master can annoyingly spawn creatures every two or three turns, slowing down progress for the players.  This game is also a little fiddly as well.  I prefer Doom to Descent because Doom is more streamlined and also has a shorter playing time.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
A little hard for me to rate this since the only time I played was with RTL and with it, it was quite good. The main game is mostly the same and I liked it a good bit but rate RTL higher, below are my comments for that game which largely apply here also:

------------------------------------------------------

initial rating 1 play so far

As a long time D&D player I was quite looking forward to just regular Descent for some time and when I read about the RTL expansion I had to try it asap. I never did play the original alone so I won't be able to comment on the improvement that it obviously makes. I also have only played one other real Dungeon crawl boardgame which was Heroquest. No comparison to make there, HQ is very dated and really suited for ages 7 to 12 IMO. End of Disclaimer. 

This was a pretty enjoyable  system to play so far. I really liked how you had one main board as the 'world' you are in and to explore so to speak, and then encounters were fought off that board onto a whole new board which is the same kind of modular type dungeons as the original(so I was told).

I was a little disapointed with the characters limited abilities, attributes etc coming from D&D however there were choices to be made on a starting character and then equipment and also skills as well with more skills being able to be purchased in the future. 

The big appeal to the expansion other than what I think I mentioned above is of course the shorter play time and ability to easily save a game and come back to it later, one of the reasons I never rushed to play the original, if I had 4 or 5 hours to spare I might as well play D&D. Well this is a great feature and they have even included "tuck boxes" for each character and the DM so you can pack away all your currect health, skills, potions, whatever you have and then just easily open it up and dump it out next time ready for play. 

I like the idea of competing against a 'live person' which is obviously more challenging than random encounters or the like however I still really have to play Warhammer Quest as I have read a lot of good things about it and it looks to play well with only 2 players (I beleive you can have a DM or also have the option to NOT have one as well). Could be the only other real competition for this now along with maybe Magic Realm. 

Looking forward to more plays

RATING: 6.1
Excellent game. I don't mind the length as some do, it cut too many corners to shorten game play time and I can appreciate that. It's VERY similar to Doom, at least at it's core and I do like this slightly more than Doom. 

I can also see why some people may prefer this dungeon crawl over Runebound. My games of Runebound (and I use all the expansion I can) end of with me having more cards than I know what to do with. I think the basic movement of Descent is just so much better than Runebound lousy movement system, my one major gripe with RB. However I really enjoy the character development of RB.  I would love to try Descent as a campaign even though we couldn't even get though my first game.  

All in all, if I had to choose all over...Runebound and all of it's expansions or Descent and all of it's expansions, I'd have a very difficult time choosing. I also own Doom, which influences my desire to own Descent.  It's worth owning, there's no doubt about that, it's just that owning Doom I have the core mechanics covered and owning Runebound scratches my dungeon crawl itch. 

UPDATE:  Yeah it's a great game but I'm bumping the rating down a bit more.  It's becuase a number of games have come such a Ravenloft and Thunderstone that further scratch the dungeon crawl itch without going on all day.

RATING: 5
2016-03-14

Initial Rating: 7.5 (January 2012)

I played Descent: Journeys in the Dark a long time ago, so I don’t remember all the game nuances. 

I remember I thought the game´s miniatures were very impressive, along with Doom´s, one of the best I have seen at the time. I also remember I thought the game to be very long for what it offered. I don’t mind long games, but Descent becomes repetitive rather soon. In theory I love that the Overlord has to setup each room when a hero reaches it, but in practice this adds to the playtime and it becomes tedious after doing it a lot of times.

At the time, I thought the game was great, perhaps the best dungeon crawler ever made but I have changed a lot in these four years. I have drifted away from Ameritrash games and now I enjoy more Eurogames, due to this, I’m downgrading Descent’s rating to one that matches my current feelings for the game with BGG´s rating system.

Bottom line, my previous self, loved the game, but my current self don’t want to play Descent: Journeys in the Dark, mainly because it requires a long of time to play it and it is very repetitive. I bought a couple of expansions in the past years, but I already sold them.

Current Rating: 5.0

RATING: 5

RATING: 5
Dungeon crawl where player characters better themselves through the acquisition of gear from chests that the monsters have left lying about. Conflict resolution is reasonably quick and gratifying (rolling many dice is fun), but the goal of a fast pace is often undermined through tactical deliberations, the use of Diabloesque town portals and trade, and the critical importance of determining line of sight to every god-forsaken corner. Completing the first scenario with five players (of which only the Overlord was familiar with the game) and minimal spawning took some five hours, which is a bit much for something that, viewing the events from the perspective of the hero players, could have happened in the span of minutes in a computer game, and in an RPG would be looked back on by many as a bad session. Also I can imagine that it would be stunningly anticlimactic if the Overlord ever won, but from what I read that tends to happen early or not at all.

RATING: 10
Rated after many plays:

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
I really, really want to like Descent more than I do. Descent has a lot of things going for it that are peripheral to the mechanics of the game itself - an enveloping mood, interesting characters, and a nice, old-school Dungeon Crawl that invokes some of the best D&D games out there.

The problem, though, is that the mechanics deviate so far from the mood that most games I've played of it end in not a sense of accomplishment (like a good D&D dungeon crawl) but in disappointment. In D&D the game-master isn't inherently antagonistic and is almost unlimited in the toolbox s/he can bring; in Descent the game-master is inherently antagonistic (since there is a winner and loser in Descent, unlike D&D). Because these fundamentals are so very different the game leaves a very bad taste in my mouth nearly every time I play it.

This means that, if the Overlord knows what they're doing and is trying to win the game ends up taking a very different form, focused far more on getting treasure, exiting quickly,  and _not_ fighting than on enjoying the scenery and mood which are the best points of the game.

So the game is more fun if it's played like D&D ... which probably means you should just play D&D instead. On the plus side the Descent tiles work great for 4e!

I guess the big problem is that now I have to seriously consider investing in this beast.  
I have Heroquest already, so maybe in a few years I'll see how my kids go for that game and then spring Descent on them as the next level.

It´s a kind of "no item = starting character ; one silver item = god mode" ... if you have your first silver items (which happens early enough) the group improves so much the overlord won´t have a real chance anymore...
